<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;background:#f8f8f8; text-align:center;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:US_2016_presidential_elections_series" title="Template:US 2016 presidential elections series"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:US_2016_presidential_elections_series" title="Template talk:US 2016 presidential elections series"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:US_2016_presidential_elections_series&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></
li>
</ul>

<p>The <b>United States presidential election of 2016,</b> <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">constitutionally</a> prescribed to occur on Tuesday, November 8, 2016, will be the 58th quadrennial <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election" title="United States presidential election">U.S. presidential election</a>. Voters will select <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">presidential electors</a> who in turn will elect a new president and vice president through the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Term_limits_in_the_United_States" title="Term limits in the United States">term limit</a> established in the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-second_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-second Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twenty-second Amendment to the 
United States Constitution</a> prevents the incumbent president, <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a>, from being elected to a third term. Assuming President Obama and Vice President <a href="/wiki/Joe_Biden" title="Joe Biden">Joe Biden</a> serve out the remainder of their respective terms, the 2016 election will determine the 45th <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> and 48th <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President of the United States</a>.</p>
<p>The series of <a hr
ef="/wiki/United_States_presidential_primary" title="United States presidential primary">presidential primary elections and caucuses</a> took place between February 1 and June 14, 2016, staggered among the 50 states, the District of Columbia and U.S. territories. This nominating process is also an indirect election, where voters cast ballots for a slate of delegates to a political party's <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_nominating_convention" title="United States presidential nominating convention">nominating convention</a>, who then in turn elect their party's presidential nominee. The <a href="/wiki/2016_Republican_National_Convention" title="2016 Republican National Convention">2016 Republican National Convention</a> will take place from July 18-21, 2016 in <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland, Ohio</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/2016_Democratic_National_Convention" title="2016 Democratic National Convention">2016 Democratic National Convention</a> will take place from July 25-28,
 2016 in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia, Pennsylvania</a>.</p>
<p>Businessman and <a href="/wiki/The_Apprentice_(TV_series)" title="The Apprentice (TV series)">reality television</a> personality <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> became the <a href="/wiki/Presumptive_nominee" class="mw-redirect" title="Presumptive nominee">presumptive</a> presidential nominee of the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> on May 3, 2016 after his win in the <a href="/wiki/Indiana_Republican_primary,_2016" title="Indiana Republican primary, 2016">Indiana primary</a><sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> and the subsequent suspensions<sup id="cite_ref-suspend_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suspend-3">[a]</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/Ted_Cruz" title="Ted Cruz">Ted Cruz</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Kasich" title="John Kasich">John Kasich</a>'s campaig
ns. Former Secretary of State and New York Senator <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> became the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries, 2016">presumptive presidential nominee</a> of the Democratic Party in the general election on June 6, 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> Vermont Senator <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Sanders" title="Bernie Sanders">Bernie Sanders</a>' campaign has stated he will assist in efforts to defeat Trump, but he has neither withdrawn from the race nor endorsed Clinton,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> although he stated that he will vote for Clinton in the general election.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Various <a href="/wiki/United_States_third_party_and_independent_pres
idential_candidates,_2016" title="United States third party and independent presidential candidates, 2016">third party and independent presidential candidates</a> will also contest the election, of which two have currently obtained enough <a href="/wiki/Ballot_access" title="Ballot access">ballot access</a> to mathematically have a chance of winning the presidency and have been <a href="/wiki/Nationwide_opinion_polling_for_the_United_States_presidential_election,_2016" title="Nationwide opinion polling for the United States presidential election, 2016">featured in major national polls</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_Party_(United_States)" title="Libertarian Party (United States)">Libertarian Party</a> nominee, former <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_Mexico" title="Governor of New Mexico">Governor of New Mexico</a> <a href="/wiki/Gary_Johnson" title="Gary Johnson">Gary Johnson</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_the_United_States" title="Green Party of the United States">Green Party</a> presumpt
ive no
minee <a href="/wiki/Jill_Stein" title="Jill Stein">Jill Stein</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup></p>
<div class="toclimit-4">

<ul>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Background"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Background</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election#Procedure" title="United States presidential election">United States presidential election §&#160;Procedure</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Article_Two_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Clause_5:_Qualifications_for_office" title="Article Two of the United States Constitution">Article Two</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a> provides that the President and Vice President of the United States must be <a href="/wiki/Natural-born-citizen_clause" title="Natural-born-citizen clause">natural-born citizens</a> of the United States at least 35 
years old, and a resident of the United States for a period of at least 14 years. Candidates for the presidency typically seek the nomination of one of the political parties of the United States, in which case each party devises a method (such as a <a href="/wiki/Primary_election" title="Primary election">primary election</a>) to choose the candidate the party deems best suited to run for the position. The primary elections are usually <a href="/wiki/Indirect_election" title="Indirect election">indirect elections</a> where voters cast ballots for a slate of party delegates pledged to a particular candidate. The party's delegates then officially nominate a candidate to run on the party's behalf. The general election in November is also an indirect election, where voters cast ballots for a slate of members of the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a>; these electors in turn directly elect the President and Vice President.</p>
<p>O
bama, 
a <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a> and former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senator</a> from <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, is ineligible to seek reelection to a third term due to restrictions of the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-second_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-second Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twenty-second Amendment</a>; in accordance with Section I of the <a href="/wiki/Twentieth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twentieth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twentieth Amendment</a>, his term expires at 12:00 noon on January 20, 2017.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2008_presidential_election">2008 presidential election</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2008" title="United States presidential election, 2008">2008 election</a>, 
Obama was elected president, defeating the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> nominee, Senator <a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a> of Arizona, with 52.9% of the popular vote and 68% of the electoral vote,<sup id="cite_ref-house_floor_summary_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-house_floor_summary-10">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2012_election_results_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012_election_results-11">[10]</a></sup> succeeding two-term Republican President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>, the former <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Texas" title="Governor of Texas">Governor of Texas</a>. Since the end of 2009, Obama's first year in office, polling companies such as <a href="/wiki/Gallup_(company)" title="Gallup (company)">Gallup</a> have found Obama's <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_approval_rating" title="United States presidential approval 
rating">approval ratings</a> to be between 40-50%.<sup id="cite_ref-Galluppolls_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galluppolls-12">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> Analysts such as <a href="/wiki/Larry_Sabato" title="Larry Sabato">Larry Sabato</a> have noted that Obama's approval ratings could impact the 2016 campaign, either helping or hurting Clinton.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010_midterm_elections">2010 midterm elections</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_elections,_2010" title="United States elections, 2010">2010 midterm elections</a>, the Democratic Party suffered significant losses in Congress; the Republicans gained 63 seats in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_2010" title="United States House of Representatives electi
ons, 2010">House of Representatives</a> – taking back control of the chamber in the process – and six seats in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_elections,_2010" title="United States Senate elections, 2010">Senate</a>, though short of achieving a majority. As a result of the Republicans' recapture of the House after losing it to the Democrats in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_elections,_2006" title="United States elections, 2006">2006 midterm elections</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Boehner" title="John Boehner">John Boehner</a> became the 53rd <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">Speaker of the House of Representatives</a>, making Obama the first President in <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1994" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1994">16 years</a> to lose the House of Representatives in the first half of his first term in an election that was char
acteri
zed by the economy's slow recovery, and the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Tea_Party_movement" title="Tea Party movement">Tea Party movement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2012_presidential_election">2012 presidential election</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2012" title="United States presidential election, 2012">2012 presidential election</a>, Obama defeated former <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Massachusetts" title="Governor of Massachusetts">Governor of Massachusetts</a> <a href="/wiki/Mitt_Romney" title="Mitt Romney">Mitt Romney</a> with 51.1% of the popular vote and 61.7% of the electoral vote, extending his already historic presidency in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-President_Map_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-President_Map-17">[16]</a></sup> Meanwhile, despite minor losses, Republicans retained their majority of seats in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Repr
esentatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> while Democrats increased their majority in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2012_election_results_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012_election_results-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Speculation about the 2016 campaign began almost immediately following the 2012 campaign, with <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_(magazine)" title="New York (magazine)">New York</a></i> magazine declaring the race had begun in an article published on November 8, two days after the 2012 election.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> On the same day, <i><a href="/wiki/Politico" title="Politico">Politico</a></i> released an article predicting the 2016 general election may be between Clinton and former <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Florida" class="mw-redirect" title="Governor of Florida">Governor of Florida</a> <a href="/wiki/Jeb_Bush" title="
Jeb Bush">Jeb Bush</a>, while a <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> article named <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_New_Jersey" title="List of Governors of New Jersey">Governor of New Jersey</a> <a href="/wiki/Chris_Christie" title="Chris Christie">Chris Christie</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> Senator <a href="/wiki/Cory_Booker" title="Cory Booker">Cory Booker</a> as potential candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2014_midterm_elections">2014 midterm elections</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_elections,_2014" title="United States elections, 2014">2014 midterm elections</a>, <a href="/wiki/Voter_turnout" title="Voter turnout">voter turnout</a> was the lowest since <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_election
s,_1942" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1942">1942</a> with only 36.4% of eligible voters voting.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> As a result, not only had the Republicans retained control of the House of Representatives, <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_2014" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 2014">increasing their majority</a> to its largest level since March 4, 1929,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> but also <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_elections,_2014" title="United States Senate elections, 2014">gained a majority</a> in the Senate.</p>

<p>Former Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a>, who also served in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senate</a> and was the <a href="/wiki/First_Lady_of_the_United_States" title="First Lady of the United States">First Lady of the United States</a>, became the first Democrat to announce a major candidacy for the presidency. Clinton made the announcement on April 12, 2015 via a video message.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> While <a h
ref="/wiki/Nationwide_opinion_polling_for_the_Democratic_Party_2016_presidential_primaries" title="Nationwide opinion polling for the Democratic Party 2016 presidential primaries">Nationwide opinion polls</a> in 2015 indicated that Clinton is the <a href="/wiki/Front-runner" title="Front-runner">front-runner</a> for the 2016 Democratic presidential nomination, she faces challenges from <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a> Senator <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Sanders" title="Bernie Sanders">Bernie Sanders</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Sanders_leading_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanders_leading-24">[23]</a></sup> who became the second candidate when he made a formal announcement on April 30, 2015 that he was running for the Democratic nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> September 2015 polling numbers indicated a narrowing gap between Clinton and Sanders.<sup id="cite_ref-Sanders_leading_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sander
s_lead
ing-24">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> On May 30, 2015, former <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Maryland" title="Governor of Maryland">Governor of Maryland</a> <a href="/wiki/Martin_O%27Malley" title="Martin O'Malley">Martin O'Malley</a> was the third candidate to enter the race.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> Four days later, on June 3, 2015, <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Chafee" title="Lincoln Chafee">Lincoln Chafee</a>, former Independent Governor and Republican Senator of <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a>, became the fourth Democrat to announce his candidacy.<sup id="cite_ref-DelReal.2C_Jose_A_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DelReal.2C_Jose_A-29">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> On July 2, 2015, former <a href="/wiki/Virginia" titl
e="Virginia">Virginia</a> Senator <a href="/wiki/Jim_Webb" title="Jim Webb">Jim Webb</a> became the fifth Democrat to announce his candidacy.<sup id="cite_ref-Announces_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Announces-31">[30]</a></sup> On September 6, 2015, former Harvard law professor <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Lessig" title="Lawrence Lessig">Lawrence Lessig</a> became the sixth and final Democrat to announce his candidacy.<sup id="cite_ref-Lessig_running_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lessig_running-32">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 20, 2015, Webb announced his withdrawal from the Democratic primaries, and explored a potential Independent run.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> The next day, Biden, the incumbent Vice President and former <a href="/wiki/U.S._Senators" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Senators">U.S. Senator</a> from <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a>, opted not to run, ending months of speculation, stating, "Whil
e I will not be a candidate, I will not be silent."<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> Two days later, on October 23, 2015, Chafee withdrew, stating that he hoped for "an end to the endless wars and the beginning of a new era for the United States and humanity."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> On November 2, 2015, after failing to qualify for the second officially-sanctioned DNC debate after adoption of a rule change negated polls which before might have necessitated his inclusion in said debate, Lessig withdrew as well, narrowing the field to Clinton, O'Malley, and Sanders heading into the Iowa Caucus.<sup id="cite_ref-outofrace_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-outofrace-37">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>On February 1, 2016, in an extremely close contest, Clinton won the <a href="/wiki/Iowa_Democratic_caucuses,_2016" title="Iowa Democratic c
aucuses, 2016">Iowa caucuses</a> by a margin of 0.2% over Sanders. Winning no delegates in the contest, O'Malley withdrew from the presidential race that day. On February 9, 2016, Sanders bounced back to win the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="New Hampshire Democratic primary, 2016">New Hampshire primary</a> with 60% of the vote. In the remaining two February contests, Clinton won the <a href="/wiki/Nevada_Democratic_caucuses,_2016" class="mw-redirect" title="Nevada Democratic caucuses, 2016">Nevada caucuses</a> with 53% of the vote and scored a decisive victory in the <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="South Carolina Democratic primary, 2016">South Carolina primary</a> with 73% of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 1, 2016, 11 states participated in the first of four "<a href="/wiki/Super_Tuesday" tit
le="Su
per Tuesday">Super Tuesday</a>" primaries, with Clinton winning <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a> for a total of 504 pledged delegates, while Sanders won <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a> and his home state of <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a> to garner a total of 340 delegates by the end of the night. On the weekend of March 5, 2016, Sanders racked up victories in <a href="/wiki/Kansas_Democratic_caucuses,_2016" title="Kansas Democratic caucuses, 2016">Kansas</a>, <a href="/w
iki/Nebraska_Democratic_caucuses,_2016" title="Nebraska Democratic caucuses, 2016">Nebraska</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maine_Democratic_caucuses,_2016" title="Maine Democratic caucuses, 2016">Maine</a> with 15- to 30-point margins, while Clinton won the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="Louisiana Democratic primary, 2016">Louisiana primary</a> with 71% of the vote. On March 8, 2016, despite never having a lead in the <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="Michigan Democratic primary, 2016">Michigan primary</a>, Sanders scored a stunning upset, winning by a small margin of 1.5% and outperforming polls by over 19 points, while, as expected, Clinton won 83% of the vote in <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="Mississippi Democratic primary, 2016">Mississippi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> On March 15, 2016, the second of four "Super Tuesday" primaries, Clinton won <a href="/wiki/Florida_Democratic_prim
ary,_2016" title="Florida Democratic primary, 2016">Florida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="Illinois Democratic primary, 2016">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="Missouri Democratic primary, 2016">Missouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="North Carolina Democratic primary, 2016">North Carolina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="Ohio Democratic primary, 2016">Ohio</a>. Between March 22 and April 9, 2016, Sanders won six caucuses in <a href="/wiki/Idaho_Democratic_caucuses,_2016" title="Idaho Democratic caucuses, 2016">Idaho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah_Democratic_caucuses,_2016" title="Utah Democratic caucuses, 2016">Utah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Democratic_caucuses,_2016" title="Alaska Democratic caucuses, 2016">Alaska</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_Democratic_caucuses,_2016" title="Hawaii Democratic caucuses, 2016">Hawaii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_Democratic_caucuses,_2016" title="Was
hington Democratic caucuses, 2016">Washington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wyoming_Democratic_caucuses,_2016" title="Wyoming Democratic caucuses, 2016">Wyoming</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="Wisconsin Democratic primary, 2016">Wisconsin primary</a>, while Clinton won the <a href="/wiki/Arizona_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="Arizona Democratic primary, 2016">Arizona primary</a>. By this stage, Clinton had won 1,305 pledged delegates to Sanders' 1,099.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 19, 2016, Clinton won the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="New York Democratic primary, 2016">New York primary</a> with 58% of the vote. On April 26, 2016, in the third of four "Super Tuesday" primaries dubbed the "Acela primary," she won contests in <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="Connecticut Democratic primary, 2016">Connecticut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delaware_Democratic_p
rimary
,_2016" title="Delaware Democratic primary, 2016">Delaware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="Maryland Democratic primary, 2016">Maryland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="Pennsylvania Democratic primary, 2016">Pennsylvania</a> while Sanders won <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="Rhode Island Democratic primary, 2016">Rhode Island</a>. On May 3, 2016, Sanders pulled off another surprise win in the Indiana primary with a 5-point margin of victory<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> and he proceeded to claim <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="West Virginia Democratic primary, 2016">West Virginia</a> on May 10, 2016 with a 15-point lead, while Clinton won the <a href="/wiki/Guam_Democratic_caucus,_2016" title="Guam Democratic caucus, 2016">Guam caucus</a> on May 7, 2016.</p>
<p>As of May 10, 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:
none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_2016&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, of the 78% of pledged delegates allocated in primaries and caucuses so far, Clinton has won 54% to Sanders' 46%.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-43">[42]</a></sup> Out of the 714 <a href="/wiki/List_of_Democratic_Party_superdelegates,_2016" title="List of Democratic Party superdelegates, 2016">unpledged delegates or "superdelegates"</a> who will vote in the <a href="/wiki/2016_Democratic_National_Convention" title="2016 Democratic National Convention">convention in July</a>, Clinton has received endorsements from 505 (71%), while Sanders has received 41 (6%).<sup id="cite_ref-:0_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-43">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 6, 2016, the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> and <a href="/wiki/NBC_News" title="NBC News">NBC News</a> stated that Clint
on had become the <a href="/wiki/Presumptive_nominee" class="mw-redirect" title="Presumptive nominee">presumptive nominee</a> after reaching the required number of delegates, including both pledged and unpledged delegates (superdelegates), to secure the nomination, becoming the <a href="/wiki/List_of_American_women%27s_firsts" title="List of American women's firsts">first woman</a> to ever clinch the nomination of any major political party in the United States in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> On June 7, 2016, Clinton officially secured a majority of pledged delegates after winning in the California and New Jersey primaries. She won the final primary in Washington, D.C. on June 14, 2016. Sanders has not officially dropped out of the race, but announced on June 16, 2016 that his main goal in the coming months would be to work with Clinton to defeat Trump in the general election.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></
sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Withdrawn_candidates">Withdrawn candidates</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_candidates,_2016" title="Democratic Party presidential candidates, 2016">Democratic Party presidential candidates, 2016</a></div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Martin_O%27Malley" title="Martin O'Malley">Martin O'Malley</a>, 61st <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Maryland" title="Governor of Maryland">Governor of Maryland</a> <small>(2007–2015)</small>. Suspended<sup id="cite_ref-suspend_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suspend-3">[a]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Martin_O%27Malley_presidential_campaign,_2016" t
itle="
Martin O'Malley presidential campaign, 2016">campaign</a> on February 1, 2016<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> and endorsed <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Lessig" title="Lawrence Lessig">Lawrence Lessig</a>, Harvard Law Professor (2009-2016). Suspended <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Lessig_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Lawrence Lessig presidential campaign, 2016">campaign</a> on November 2, 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-outofrace_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-outofrace-37">[36]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Chafee" title="Lincoln Chafee">Lincoln Chafee</a>, 74th <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Rhode_Island" class="mw-redirect" title="Governor of Rhode Island">Governor of Rhode Island</a> <small>(2011–2015)</small> and former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. S
enator</a> <small>(1999–2007)</small>. Suspended <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Chafee_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Lincoln Chafee presidential campaign, 2016">campaign</a> on October 23, 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jim_Webb" title="Jim Webb">Jim Webb</a>, former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senator</a> <small>(2007–2013)</small>. Suspended <a href="/wiki/Jim_Webb_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Jim Webb presidential campaign, 2016">campaign</a> on October 20, 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<p>A total of 17 major candidates entered the race starting March 23, 2015, when Senator <a href="/wiki/Ted_Cruz" title="Ted Cruz">Ted Cruz</a> of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> was the first to formally announce his candidacy: former Governor <a href="/wiki/Jeb_Bush" title="Jeb Bush">Jeb Bush</a> of Florida, retired neurosurgeon <a href="/wiki/Ben_Carson" title="Ben Carson">Ben Carson</a> of Maryland, Governor <a href="/wiki/Chris_Christie" title="Chris Christie">Chris Christie</a> of New Jersey, businesswoman <a href="/wiki/Carly_Fiorina" title="Carly Fiorina">Carly Fiorina</a> of California, former Governor <a href="/wiki/Jim_Gilmore" title="Jim Gilmore">Jim Gilmore</a> of Virginia, Senator <a href="/wiki/Lindsey_Graham" title="Lindsey Graham">Lindsey Graham</a> of South Carolina, former Governor <a href="/wiki/Mike_Huckabee" title="Mike Huckabee">Mike Huckabee</a> of Arkansas, former Governor <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Jindal" title="Bobby Jindal">Bob
by Jindal</a> of Louisiana, Governor <a href="/wiki/John_Kasich" title="John Kasich">John Kasich</a> of Ohio, former Governor <a href="/wiki/George_Pataki" title="George Pataki">George Pataki</a> of New York, Senator <a href="/wiki/Rand_Paul" title="Rand Paul">Rand Paul</a> of Kentucky, former Governor <a href="/wiki/Rick_Perry" title="Rick Perry">Rick Perry</a> of Texas, Senator <a href="/wiki/Marco_Rubio" title="Marco Rubio">Marco Rubio</a> of Florida, former Senator <a href="/wiki/Rick_Santorum" title="Rick Santorum">Rick Santorum</a> of Pennsylvania, businessman <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> of New York and Governor <a href="/wiki/Scott_Walker" title="Scott Walker">Scott Walker</a> of Wisconsin. This was the largest presidential primary field for any political party in American history.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Prior to the <a href="/wiki/Iowa_Republican_caucuses,_2016" title="Iowa Republican caucuses, 2
016">I
owa caucuses</a> on February 1, 2016, Perry, Walker, Jindal, Graham and Pataki withdrew due to low polling numbers. Despite leading many polls in Iowa, Trump came in second to Cruz, after which Huckabee, Paul and Santorum withdrew due to poor performances at the ballot box. Following a sizable victory for Trump in the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Republican_primary,_2016" title="New Hampshire Republican primary, 2016">New Hampshire primary</a>, Christie, Fiorina and Gilmore abandoned the race. Bush followed suit after scoring fourth place to Trump, Rubio and Cruz in <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina_Republican_primary,_2016" title="South Carolina Republican primary, 2016">South Carolina</a>. On March 1, 2016, the first of four "<a href="/wiki/Super_Tuesday" title="Super Tuesday">Super Tuesday</a>" primaries, Rubio won his first contest in Minnesota, Cruz won Alaska, Oklahoma and his home of Texas and Trump won the other seven states that voted. Failing to gain traction, Carson suspended his campaign a few days la
ter.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> On March 15, 2016, the second of four "Super Tuesday" primaries, Kasich won his only contest in his home state of Ohio and Trump won five primaries including Florida. Rubio suspended his campaign after losing his home state,<sup id="cite_ref-Rubio_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubio-62">[61]</a></sup> but retained a large share of his delegates for the national convention, which he plans to release to Trump.<sup id="cite_ref-Rubio_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubio-62">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between March 16 and May 3, 2016, only three candidates remained in the race: Trump, Cruz and Kasich. Cruz won most delegates in four Western contests and in Wisconsin, keeping a credible path to denying Trump the nomination on first ballot with 1,237 delegates. However, Trump scored landslide victories in New York and five Northeastern states in April and he grabbed all 57 delegates in the Indiana primary of May
 3, 2016. Without any further chances of forcing a contested convention, both Cruz<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> and Kasich<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> suspended their campaigns. Trump remained the only active candidate and was declared the presumptive Republican nominee by <a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Committee" title="Republican National Committee">Republican National Committee</a> chairman <a href="/wiki/Reince_Priebus" title="Reince Priebus">Reince Priebus</a> on the evening of May 3, 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup></p>

n February 1, 2016<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> and later endorsed <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/George_Pataki" title="George Pataki">George Pataki</a>, 53rd <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_York" title="Governor of New York">Governor of New York</a> <small>(1995-2006)</small>. Suspended <a href="/wiki/George_Pataki_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="George Pataki presidential campaign, 2016">campaign</a> on December 29, 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> and initially endorsed <a href="/wiki/Marco_Rubio" title="Marco Rubio">Marco Rubio</a> on January 26, 2016<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> before endorsing <a href="/wiki/John_Kasich" title="John Kasich">John Kasich</a> on April 14, 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a 
href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lindsey_Graham" title="Lindsey Graham">Lindsey Graham</a>, senior <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senator</a> from <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> <small>(2003-present)</small>. Suspended <a href="/wiki/Lindsey_Graham_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Lindsey Graham presidential campaign, 2016">campaign</a> on December 21, 2015 and initially endorsed <a href="/wiki/Jeb_Bush" title="Jeb Bush">Jeb Bush</a> on January 15, 2016 before endorsing <a href="/wiki/Ted_Cruz" title="Ted Cruz">Ted Cruz</a> on March 17, 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bobby_Jindal" title="B
obby Jindal">Bobby Jindal</a>, 55th <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Louisiana" class="mw-redirect" title="Governor of Louisiana">Governor of Louisiana</a> <small>(2008–2016)</small>. Suspended <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Jindal_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Bobby Jindal presidential campaign, 2016">campaign</a> on November 17, 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> and endorsed Marco Rubio,<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> then later endorsed <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Scott_Walker" title="Scott Walker">Scott Walker</a>, 45th <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Wisconsin" title="Governor of Wisconsin">Governor of Wisconsin</a> <small>(2011-present)</small>. Suspended <a href="/wiki/Scott_Walker_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Scott Walker presidential campaign, 2016">campaig
n</a> 
on September 21, 2015<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> and initially endorsed <a href="/wiki/Ted_Cruz" title="Ted Cruz">Ted Cruz</a> on March 29, 2016<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> before endorsing <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> on June 2, 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rick_Perry" title="Rick Perry">Rick Perry</a>, 47th <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Texas" title="Governor of Texas">Governor of Texas</a> <small>(2000-2015)</small>. Suspended <a href="/wiki/Rick_Perry_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Rick Perry presidential campaign, 2016">campaign</a> on September 11, 2015<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> and initially endorsed <a href="/wiki/Ted_Cruz" title="Ted Cruz">Ted Cruz</a> on January 25, 2016<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#
cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> then later endorsed <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vice_presidential_possibilities_2">Vice presidential possibilities</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_vice_presidential_candidate_selection,_2016" title="Republican Party vice presidential candidate selection, 2016">Republican Party vice presidential candidate selection, 2016</a></div>
<p>The following people have been cited as possible running mates for Donald Trump:<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="refer
ence"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chris_Christie" title="Chris Christie">Chris Christie</a>, Governor of <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Kasich" title="John Kasich">John Kasich</a>, Governor of <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a></li>

Maryland">Maryland</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Ted_Cruz" title="Ted Cruz">Ted Cruz</a>, United States Senator from <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carly_Fiorina" title="Carly Fiorina">Carly Fiorina</a>, businesswoman from <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a></li>

Flynn</a>, retired <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a> <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_general_(United_States)" title="Lieutenant general (United States)">lieutenant general</a> from <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_third_parties">Major third parties</span></h2>
<div rol
e="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_third_party_and_independent_presidential_candidates,_2016" title="United States third party and independent presidential candidates, 2016">United States third party and independent presidential candidates, 2016</a></div>
<p>Parties in this section have obtained ballot access in enough states to theoretically obtain the minimum number of electoral votes needed to win the election. Unless otherwise specified, individuals included in this section have taken one or more of the following actions: formally announced their candidacy for the presidential nomination of a <a href="/wiki/Third_party_(United_States)" title="Third party (United States)">third party</a>; formally announced intention to run as an <a href="/wiki/Independent_politician" title="Independent politician">independent</a> candidate and obtained enough ballot access to win the election; filed as a third party or non-affiliated candidate with the FEC (for other than exploratory purpo
ses). Within each party, candidates are listed alphabetically by surname.</p>

<p>On May 29, 2016, Johnson won the <a href="/wiki/2016_Libertarian_National_Convention" title="2016 Libertarian National Convention">Libertarian nomination</a> on the second ballot with 55.8% of the delegates.<sup id="cite_ref-JohnsonwinsNomination_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JohnsonwinsNomination-124">[123]</a></sup> The convention selected former Republican Governor of Massachusetts <a href="/wiki/William_Weld" title
="William Weld">William Weld</a> as his running mate.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_third_parties_and_independents">Other third parties and independents</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_third_party_and_independent_presidential_candidates,_2016" title="United States third party and independent presidential candidates, 2016">United States third party and independent presidential candidates, 201
6</a></div>
<p>Parties and candidates in this section have attained ballot access in one or more states but have yet to obtain access to the minimum number of electoral votes needed to theoretically win the election. Unless otherwise specified, individuals included in this section have taken one or more of the following actions: formally announced their candidacy for the presidential nomination of a <a href="/wiki/Minor_party" title="Minor party">minor party</a>; formally announced intention to run as an <a href="/wiki/Independent_politician" title="Independent politician">independent</a> candidate; filed as a minor party or non-affiliated candidate with the FEC (for other than exploratory purposes). Candidates are listed by party and then alphabetically by surname.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Delta_Party">American Delta Party</span></h3>
<p><small>Ballot access to 5 electoral votes: New Mexico.</small><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup></p>
<ul
>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rocky_De_La_Fuente" title="Rocky De La Fuente">Rocky De La Fuente</a>, businessman from <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>. Vice Presidential nominee: Vacant</li>
</ul>

(politician)">Scott Bradley</a> from Utah.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_battleground_states">Potential battleground states</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Swing_state" title="Swing state">Swing state</a></div>
<p>In every state except <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a>, the winner of the popular vote in the state <a href="/wiki/Winner-takes-all" class="mw-redirect" title="Winner-takes-all">wins</a> all of the <a
 href="/wiki/Electoral_vote" class="mw-redirect" title="Electoral vote">electoral votes</a> of the state (although state legislatures can, by law, change how <a href="/wiki/Electoral_college_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Electoral college (United States)">electors</a> are elected).<sup id="cite_ref-Congressional_district_method_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Congressional_district_method-149">[148]</a></sup> Maine and Nebraska use the "congressional district method", in which the winner of the state receives two electoral votes and candidates receive additional electoral votes for each <a href="/wiki/Congressional_district" title="Congressional district">congressional district</a> that they win. Recent presidential campaigns have generally focused their resources on a relatively small number of competitive states.<sup id="cite_ref-BDoherty_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BDoherty-150">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AAbram_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-
AAbram-151">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>Potential <a href="/wiki/Battleground_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Battleground states">battleground states</a> include <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a> and <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DBalz_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DBalz-152">[151]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sabato2015predictions_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#c
ite_note-sabato2015predictions-153">[152]</a></sup> Other potential Democratic targets include Nebraska's second congressional district, <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DBalz_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DBalz-152">[151]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sabato2015predictions_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sabato2015predictions-153">[152]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Republicans may also target Maine's second congressional district, <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sabato2015predictions_153-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sabato2015predictions-153">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a hre
f="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup> Other states may also become competitive if the close races of 2016 differ from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2012#Close_races" title="United States presidential election, 2012">close races of the 2012 election</a>, or if 2016 becomes a <a href="/wiki/Landslide_election" class="mw-redirect" title="Landslide election">landslide election</a>.</p>
<p>Several sites and individuals publish predictions of competitive states. The predictions assign ratings to each state, with the rating indicating the predicted advantage that a party has in winning that state. Most election predictors use "<u>tossup</u>" to indicate that neither party has an advantage, "<u>lean</u>" to indicate that one party has a slight advantage, "<u>likely" or "favored</u>" to indicate that one party has a significant but not insurmountable advantage, and "<u>safe" or "solid</u>" to indicate that one party has a near-certain chance of victory. Some predictions also include a "<u>tilt</
u>" rating that indicates that one party has an advantage that is not quite as strong as the "lean" rating would indicate. All states classified with at least one rating of anything other than "safe" or "solid" are listed below. The state's <a href="/wiki/Cook_PVI" class="mw-redirect" title="Cook PVI">Cook PVI</a> and the last year that the state "swung" (i.e. voted for the party that did not win the state in 2012) are also included in the table. Maine and Nebraska's competitive districts are also included in the table.</p>

Sites of the 2016 general elect
ion debates</div>

<p>The <a href="/wiki/Commission_on_Presidential_Debates" title="Commission on Presidential Debates">Commission on Presidential Debates</a> (CPD), a non-profit bipartisan organization controlled by the Republican and Democratic parties, is slated to host <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election_debates" title="United States presidential election debates">debates</a> between presidential nominees in addition to their vice presidential candidates who qualify for and agree to participate in the debates. According to the commission's website, to be eligible to opt to participate in the anticipated debates, "... in addition to being Constitutionally eligible, candidates must appear on a sufficient number of state ballots to have a mathematical chance of winning a majority vote in the Electoral College, and have a level of support of at least 15 percent of the national electorate as determined by five selected national public opinion polling organizations, using the average
 of those organizations' most recently publicly-reported results at the time of the determination."<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup> The three locations chosen to host the presidential debates, and the one location selected to host the vice presidential debate, were announced on September 23, 2015. However, as the time nears the party conventions, the campaign of the presumptive Republican nominee has not yet committed to participate in the debates, which have become a staple of presidential election cycles for over a half-century.<sup id="cite_ref-CPD_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CPD-173">[172]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CPD_decision_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CPD_decision-175">[174]</a></sup></p>

<p>The <a href="/wiki/Free_%26_Equal_Elections_Foundation" title="Free &amp; Equal Elections Foundation">Free &amp; Equal Elections Foundation</a> plan to host an open debate among all presidential candidates who have ballot access sufficient to represent a majority of electoral votes.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup> It is to be held at the University of Colorado Boulder's Macky Auditorium on October 25, 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup> As of May 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_2016&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the nominees
 of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a>, <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_Party_(United_States)" title="Libertarian Party (United States)">Libertarian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_the_United_States" title="Green Party of the United States">Green</a> parties qualify for this debate.</p>
